<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Unreal Engine</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>

<p>The <b>Unreal Engine</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Game_engine" title="Game engine">game engine</a> developed by <a href="/wiki/Epic_Games" title="Epic Games">Epic Games</a>, first showcased in the 1998 <a href="/wiki/First-person_shooter" title="First-person shooter">first-person shooter</a> <a href="/wiki/Video_game" title="Video game">game</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Unreal_(1998_video_game)" title="Unreal (1998 video game)">Unreal</a></i>. Although primarily developed for first-person shooters, it has been successfully used in a variety of other genres, including <a href="/wiki/Stealth_game" title="Stealth game">stealth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Massively_multiplayer_online_role-playing_game" title="Massively multiplayer online role-playing game">MMORPGs</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Role-playing_video_game" title="Role-playing video game">RPGs</a>. With its code written in <a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B" title="C++">C++</a>, the Unreal Engine features a high degree of portability and is a tool used by many game developers today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wiredUE4_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-wiredUE4-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-popular_mechanics_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-popular_mechanics-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>The current release is Unreal Engine 4, designed for <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a>'s <a href="/wiki/DirectX" title="DirectX">DirectX</a> 11 and 12<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> (for <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Microsoft Windows</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xbox_One" title="Xbox One">Xbox One</a>, <a href="/wiki/Windows_RT" title="Windows RT">Windows RT</a>); <a href="/wiki/PlayStation_4" title="PlayStation 4">GNM</a> (for <a href="/wiki/PlayStation_4" title="PlayStation 4">PlayStation 4</a>); OpenGL (for <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/MacOS" title="MacOS">macOS</a></span>, <a href="/wiki/Linux" title="Linux">Linux</a>, <a href="/wiki/IOS" title="IOS">iOS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Android_(operating_system)" title="Android (operating system)">Android</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ouya" title="Ouya">Ouya</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Windows_XP" title="Windows XP">Windows XP</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>); <a href="/wiki/Vulkan_(API)" title="Vulkan (API)">Vulkan</a> (for <a href="/wiki/Android_(operating_system)" title="Android (operating system)">Android</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>); <a href="/wiki/Metal_(API)" title="Metal (API)">Metal</a> (for <a href="/wiki/IOS" title="IOS">iOS</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>); and <a href="/wiki/JavaScript" title="JavaScript">JavaScript</a>/<a href="/wiki/WebGL" title="WebGL">WebGL</a> (for <a href="/wiki/HTML5" title="HTML5">HTML5</a> <a href="/wiki/Web_browser" title="Web browser">web browsers</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unreal_Engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unreal_Engine_1">Unreal Engine 1</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unreal_Engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Unreal Engine 1">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Unreal-GlideVoodoo1flyby.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="320" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/65/Unreal-GlideVoodoo1flyby.jpg/260px-Unreal-GlideVoodoo1flyby.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/6/65/Unreal-GlideVoodoo1flyby.jpg 1.5x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Unreal-GlideVoodoo1flyby.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Unreal_(1998_video_game)" title="Unreal (1998 video game)">Unreal</a></i> was the first game using the Unreal Engine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>

<p>Making its debut in 1998 with <i><a href="/wiki/Unreal_(1998_video_game)" title="Unreal (1998 video game)">Unreal</a></i>, the first generation Unreal Engine integrated <a href="/wiki/Rendering_(computer_graphics)" title="Rendering (computer graphics)">rendering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Collision_detection" title="Collision detection">collision detection</a>, <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence" title="Artificial intelligence">AI</a>, visibility, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computer_networking" title="Computer networking">networking</a>, scripting, and <a href="/wiki/File_system" title="File system">file system</a> management into one complete engine. Unreal Engine 1 provided an advanced software <a href="/wiki/Rasterisation" title="Rasterisation">rasterizer</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Unreal_Engine_1_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Unreal_Engine_1-16">[16]</a></sup> and a hardware-accelerated rendering path using the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glide_API" title="Glide API">Glide API</a>, specifically developed for <a href="/wiki/3dfx_Interactive" title="3dfx Interactive">3dfx</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GPU" title="GPU">GPUs</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doom2Dunia3_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Doom2Dunia3-17">[17]</a></sup> and was updated for <a href="/wiki/OpenGL" title="OpenGL">OpenGL</a> and <a href="/wiki/Direct3D" title="Direct3D">Direct3D</a>. The release of <i><a href="/wiki/Unreal_Tournament" title="Unreal Tournament">Unreal Tournament</a></i> marked great strides in both network performance and Direct3D and OpenGL support.</p>
<p>The engine became popular due to the modular engine architecture and the inclusion of a scripting language called UnrealScript, which made it easy to <a href="/wiki/Mod_(video_gaming)" title="Mod (video gaming)">mod</a>, including total conversions like <i><a href="/wiki/Tactical_Ops:_Assault_on_Terror" title="Tactical Ops: Assault on Terror">Tactical Ops</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-informitUnreal_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-informitUnreal-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>From the start, the engine was designed in a way to be extensible and improved over multiple generations of games, as creator and founder of <a href="/wiki/Epic_Games" title="Epic Games">Epic Games</a> <a href="/wiki/Tim_Sweeney_(game_developer)" title="Tim Sweeney (game developer)">Tim Sweeney</a> stated in a 1998 interview with magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Maximum_PC" title="Maximum PC">Maximum PC</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maximumPC_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-maximumPC-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unreal_Engine_2">Unreal Engine 2</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unreal_Engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Unreal Engine 2">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Killing_Floor_Biohazard1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1050" data-file-width="1680" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Killing_Floor_Biohazard1.jpg/260px-Killing_Floor_Biohazard1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Killing_Floor_Biohazard1.jpg/390px-Killing_Floor_Biohazard1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Killing_Floor_Biohazard1.jpg/520px-Killing_Floor_Biohazard1.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Killing_Floor_Biohazard1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Killing_Floor_(video_game)" title="Killing Floor (video game)">Killing Floor</a></i> was built in Unreal Engine 2.</div>

<p>The second version was released in 2002 with <i><a href="/wiki/America%27s_Army" title="America's Army">America's Army</a></i>, a free multiplayer shooter created by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">US Army</a> and financed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._government" title="U.S. government">U.S. government</a>. This generation saw the core code and rendering engine completely re-written. In addition, it featured UnrealEd 2, a <a href="/wiki/Level_editor" title="Level editor">level editor</a>, which debuted with the previous generation of the engine and was shortly followed later by UnrealEd 3, along with the Karma physics SDK. This physics engine powered the <a href="/wiki/Ragdoll_physics" title="Ragdoll physics">ragdoll physics</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Unreal_Tournament_2003" title="Unreal Tournament 2003">Unreal Tournament 2003</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Unreal_Championship" title="Unreal Championship">Unreal Championship</a></i>. Other engine elements were also updated, with improved assets as well as adding support for the <a href="/wiki/GameCube" title="GameCube">GameCube</a> and the Xbox. Support for the PlayStation 2 console was previously added in UE1. Taking Xbox aside, both GameCube and PS2 were never supported directly by Epic, support being instead farmed out to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secret_Level" title="Secret Level">Secret Level</a>. As such, third parties looking to use further Unreal Engine revisions had to do their own builds throughout the generation, as they had to in more recent years with the Wii, X360, PS3, PSP, and 3DS.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This paragraph could use a citation, not just to source the material, but also as a guide to editors who might want to clarify some of the complicated sentences here. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>UE2.5, an update to the original version of UE2, improved rendering performance and added vehicles physics, a particle system editor for UnrealEd, and 64-bit support in <i><a href="/wiki/Unreal_Tournament_2004" title="Unreal Tournament 2004">Unreal Tournament 2004</a></i>. A specialized version of UE2.5 called UE2X was used for <i><a href="/wiki/Unreal_Championship_2:_The_Liandri_Conflict" title="Unreal Championship 2: The Liandri Conflict">Unreal Championship 2: The Liandri Conflict</a></i> on the original Xbox platform. It features optimizations specific to that console. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_audio_extensions#EAX_3.0" title="Environmental audio extensions">EAX 3.0</a> is also supported for sound.</p>
<p>In March 2011, <a href="/wiki/Ubisoft" title="Ubisoft">Ubisoft</a> Montreal revealed that UE2 was successfully running on the <a href="/wiki/Nintendo_3DS" title="Nintendo 3DS">Nintendo 3DS</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unreal_Engine_3">Unreal Engine 3</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unreal_Engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Unreal Engine 3">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:272px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bioshock-infinite-columbia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="285" data-file-width="400" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/46/Bioshock-infinite-columbia.jpg/270px-Bioshock-infinite-columbia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/46/Bioshock-infinite-columbia.jpg 1.5x" width="270"/></a>

<p>The first screenshots of Unreal Engine 3 were presented in 2004,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> at which point the engine had already been in development for 18 months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Unlike Unreal Engine 2, which still supported fixed-function pipeline, Unreal Engine 3 was designed to take advantage of fully programmable shader hardware (in DirectX 9 terms, it required shader model 3.0). All lighting calculations were done per-pixel, instead of per-vertex. Unreal Engine 3 supports a gamma-correct high-dynamic range renderer.</p>
<p>Initially, Unreal Engine 3 only supported Windows, PlayStation 3, and Xbox 360 platforms, while iOS and Android were added later in 2010 (with <i><a href="/wiki/Infinity_Blade" title="Infinity Blade">Infinity Blade</a></i> being the first iOS title and <i><a href="/wiki/Dungeon_Defenders" title="Dungeon Defenders">Dungeon Defenders</a></i> the first Android title). <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/MacOS" title="MacOS">OS X</a></span> support was added in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Its renderer supports techniques including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_dynamic_range_rendering" title="High dynamic range rendering">HDRR</a>, <a href="/wiki/Per-pixel_lighting" title="Per-pixel lighting">per-pixel lighting</a>, and dynamic shadows. In October 2011, the engine was ported to support Adobe Flash Player 11 through the Stage 3D hardware-accelerated APIs. Epic has used this version of the engine for their in-house games. Aggressive licensing of this iteration has garnered a great deal of support from many prominent licensees. Epic has announced that Unreal Engine 3 runs on both Windows 8 and Windows RT.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The first released console game using Unreal Engine 3 was <i><a href="/wiki/Gears_of_War_(video_game)" title="Gears of War (video game)">Gears of War</a></i> and the first released PC game was <i><a href="/wiki/RoboBlitz" title="RoboBlitz">RoboBlitz</a></i>.</p>
<p>Throughout the lifetime of UE3, significant updates have been incorporated,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> including a <a href="/wiki/Global_illumination" title="Global illumination">global illumination</a> solver, improved destructible environments, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soft_body_dynamics" title="Soft body dynamics">soft body dynamics</a>, large <a href="/wiki/Crowd_simulation" title="Crowd simulation">crowd simulation</a>, <a href="/wiki/IPod_Touch" title="IPod Touch">iPod Touch</a> functionality,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steamworks" title="Steamworks">Steamworks</a> integration,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Global_illumination" title="Global illumination">real-time global illumination</a> solution,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> and stereoscopic 3D on Xbox 360 via <a href="/wiki/Darkworks#TriOviz_for_Games_Technology" title="Darkworks">TriOviz for Games Technology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DirectX_11" title="DirectX 11">DirectX 11</a> support was demonstrated with the <i>Samaritan</i> demo, which was built by Epic Games in a close partnership with NVIDIA, with engineers working around the country to push real-time graphics to a new high point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to the game industry, UE3 has also seen adoption by many non-gaming projects. The popular children's TV show <i><a href="/wiki/LazyTown" title="LazyTown">LazyTown</a></i> used UE3 during filming to generate virtual sets for real-time integration with footage of actors and puppets performing in front of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Green_screen" title="Green screen">green screens</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> In March 2012, the FBI licensed Epic's Unreal Engine 3 to use in a simulator for training.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-50">[50]</a></sup> The animation software "<a href="/wiki/Muvizu" title="Muvizu">Muvizu</a> Play", which was released in April 2013, uses UE3.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Epic Games is no longer supporting UE3 as of February 2015, however, it can still be used until 2020 because it can support new hardware as of 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Unreal_Development_Kit">Unreal Development Kit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unreal_Engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Unreal Development Kit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Ball_-_Screenshot_08.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1280" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/The_Ball_-_Screenshot_08.jpg/260px-The_Ball_-_Screenshot_08.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/The_Ball_-_Screenshot_08.jpg/390px-The_Ball_-_Screenshot_08.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/The_Ball_-_Screenshot_08.jpg/520px-The_Ball_-_Screenshot_08.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>

<p>While Unreal Engine 3 has been quite open for modders to work with, the ability to publish and sell games made using UE3 was restricted to licensees of the engine. However, in November 2009, Epic released a free version of UE3's <a href="/wiki/Software_development_kit" title="Software development kit">SDK</a>, called the Unreal Development Kit (UDK), that is available to the general public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>The December 2010 UDK release added support for creating iOS games and apps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> As of the September 2011 release, iOS, OS X and Windows platforms all support UDK-created games.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unreal_Engine_4">Unreal Engine 4</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unreal_Engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Unreal Engine 4">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Liandri_WIP.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="236" data-file-width="421" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ac/Liandri_WIP.jpg/260px-Liandri_WIP.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ac/Liandri_WIP.jpg/390px-Liandri_WIP.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/a/ac/Liandri_WIP.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">

<i><a href="/wiki/Unreal_Tournament_(upcoming_video_game)" title="Unreal Tournament (upcoming video game)">Unreal Tournament</a></i> is being built with Unreal Engine 4.</div>

<th scope="row" style="white-space: nowrap;">Website</th>

</tr>
</table>
<p>On August 17, 2005, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Rein_(software_executive)" title="Mark Rein (software executive)">Mark Rein</a>, the vice-president of Epic Games, revealed that Unreal Engine 4 had been in development since 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Until mid-2008, development was exclusively done by <a href="/wiki/Tim_Sweeney_(game_developer)" title="Tim Sweeney (game developer)">Tim Sweeney</a>, CEO and founder of Epic Games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The engine targets the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_video_game_consoles_(eighth_generation)" title="History of video game consoles (eighth generation)">eighth generation</a> of consoles, PCs and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tegra_K1" title="Tegra K1">Tegra K1</a>-based<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AnandTech_Tegra_K1_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-AnandTech_Tegra_K1-70">[70]</a></sup> devices running <a href="/wiki/Android_(operating_system)" title="Android (operating system)">Android</a> announced in January 2014 at <a href="/wiki/Consumer_Electronics_Show" title="Consumer Electronics Show">CES</a>.</p>
<p>In February 2012, Mark Rein said "people are going to be shocked later this year when they see Unreal Engine 4".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Unreal Engine 4 was unveiled to limited attendees at the 2012 <a href="/wiki/Game_Developers_Conference" title="Game Developers Conference">Game Developers Conference</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> and video of the engine being demonstrated by technical artist Alan "Talisman" Willard was released to the public on June 7, 2012 via <a href="/wiki/GameTrailers_TV_with_Geoff_Keighley" title="GameTrailers TV with Geoff Keighley">GameTrailers TV</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> This demo was created on a PC with triple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GeForce_500_Series" title="GeForce 500 Series">GeForce GTX 580</a> (<a href="/wiki/Scalable_Link_Interface#3-Way_SLI" title="Scalable Link Interface">tri SLI</a>) and can be run on a PC with a <a href="/wiki/GeForce" title="GeForce">GeForce</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GeForce_600_Series" title="GeForce 600 Series">GTX 680</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Interview_with_Allan_Willard_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Interview_with_Allan_Willard-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of the major features planned for UE4 was real-time global illumination using <a href="/wiki/Cone_tracing" title="Cone tracing">voxel cone tracing</a>, eliminating pre-computed lighting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> However, this feature has been replaced with a similar but less computationally-expensive algorithm prior to release for all platforms including the PC because of performance concerns on next-generation consoles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> UE4 also includes new developer features to reduce iteration time, and allows updating of <a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B" title="C++">C++</a> code while the engine is running. The new "Blueprint" visual scripting system (a successor to UE3's "Kismet"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>) allows for rapid development of game logic without using C++, and includes live debugging.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> The result is reduced iteration time, and less of a divide between technical artists, designers, and programmers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kotakuUE4_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-kotakuUE4-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>[In older engines], if you wanted to change the relationship between your weapon damage and how long it'll take to kill a creature, you may spend a couple of days iterating, but if you have to spend a lot of time waiting for a build every time, you're talking one change, waiting 15 minutes for the compile to complete, and then play the game, get to the point where you can test it, test it, exit the game, change, compile...now, since all of that can be done very quickly within the tools, it's ‘Make the change, play, when it compiles, finish, shoot the guy, and then escape, make the change, play.. the iteration time is down to 30 seconds instead of 15 minutes. Our ability to kind of roll through and see how the game is playing out is much faster.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Alan Willard, writing for <i><a href="/wiki/Kotaku" title="Kotaku">Kotaku</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kotakuUE4_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-kotakuUE4-81">[81]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>On March 19, 2014, at the <a href="/wiki/Game_Developers_Conference" title="Game Developers Conference">Game Developers Conference</a>, Epic Games released Unreal Engine 4, and all of its tools, features and complete C++ source code, to the development community through a new subscription model. Anyone can sign up for UE4 for <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Windows</a>, <a href="/wiki/MacOS" title="MacOS">OS X</a>, <a href="/wiki/IOS" title="IOS">iOS</a> and <a href="/wiki/Android_(operating_system)" title="Android (operating system)">Android</a> by paying $19 per month, plus 5% of gross revenue resulting from any commercial products built using UE4.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> CEO and founder of Epic Games, Tim Sweeney, said that the new business model is a reflection of changes in the industry. Epic Games has traditionally made its Unreal Engine available to large AAA game development teams at a cost of millions of dollars but as the industry has evolved, Epic has had to "really rethink our whole business as to how we make the engine available to teams."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> "Looking at the new shape of the industry now, we realize that's an outdated tool," Sweeney said. "Looking at the possibilities for the engine, we started from scratch and thought 'How can we make the engine available to more people?'".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> According to the Unreal Engine website, subscribers to the engine will be able to cancel and renew their subscription at any time. They will be able to retain access to UE4 tools, but will not receive access to future releases of Unreal Engine 4.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>

Interactive architectural visualization using UE4.</div>

<p>On September 3, 2014, Epic Games launched the Unreal Engine Marketplace, allowing UE4 subscribers to buy and sell community-created content of all shapes and sizes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> In addition to all of the previously released free content, the new marketplace came with a variety of asset packs including full-scale environments, props, characters, sounds, materials, animated meshes, prefab C++ code and a number of other asset types as well as free demos and tutorials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>On September 4, 2014, Epic released Unreal Engine 4 to schools and universities for free, including personal copies for students enrolled in accredited video game development, computer science, art, architecture, simulation, and visualization programs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>On February 19, 2015, Epic launched <i>Unreal Dev Grants</i>, a $5,000,000 development fund designed to provide financial grants to innovative projects being built with Unreal Engine 4.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of March 2, 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Unreal_Engine&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Unreal Engine 4 is available to everyone for free, and all future updates will be free,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> with a selective royalty schedule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UE4_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-UE4-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Oculus_VR" title="Oculus VR">Oculus VR</a> announced in October 2016 that it will cover royalty fees for all Unreal Engine titles shipping on the Oculus Store for up to the first $5 million of gross revenue per game.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> The same month Unreal Engine 4 support was confirmed for the upcoming console <a href="/wiki/Nintendo_Switch" title="Nintendo Switch">Nintendo Switch</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="UnrealScript">UnrealScript</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unreal_Engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: UnrealScript">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="infobox vevent" style="width:22em">

<th scope="row">Website</th>
<td><a class="external text" href="https://udn.epicgames.com/Three/UnrealScriptHome.html" rel="nofollow">udn.epicgames.com</a></td>

<tr>
<th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color: #eee;">Influenced by</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B" title="C++">C++</a>, <a href="/wiki/Java_(programming_language)" title="Java (programming language)">Java</a></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>UnrealScript (often abbreviated to UScript) is Unreal Engine's native scripting language used for authoring game code and <a href="/wiki/Gameplay" title="Gameplay">gameplay</a> events before the release of Unreal Engine 4. The language was designed for simple, <a href="/wiki/High-level_programming_language" title="High-level programming language">high-level</a> <a href="/wiki/Game_programming" title="Game programming">game programming</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> The UnrealScript interpreter was programmed by <a href="/wiki/Tim_Sweeney_(game_developer)" title="Tim Sweeney (game developer)">Tim Sweeney</a>, who also created an earlier game scripting language, <a href="/wiki/ZZT-oop" title="ZZT-oop">ZZT-oop</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>Similar to <a href="/wiki/Java_(programming_language)" title="Java (programming language)">Java</a>, UnrealScript is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Object-oriented" title="Object-oriented">object-oriented</a> without <a href="/wiki/Multiple_inheritance" title="Multiple inheritance">multiple inheritance</a> (classes all inherit from a common Object class), and classes are defined in individual files named for the class they define. Unlike <a href="/wiki/Java_(programming_language)" title="Java (programming language)">Java</a>, UnrealScript does not have object wrappers for primitive types. Interfaces are only supported in Unreal Engine generation 3 and a few Unreal Engine 2 games. UnrealScript supports <a href="/wiki/Operator_overloading" title="Operator overloading">operator overloading</a>, but not <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Method_overloading" title="Method overloading">method overloading</a>, except for optional parameters.</p>
<p>In March 2014 Epic announced that the Unreal Engine 4 would no longer be supporting UnrealScript, but instead support game scripting in <a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B" title="C++">C++</a>. <a href="/wiki/Visual_programming_language" title="Visual programming language">Visual scripting</a> would be supported by the Blueprints Visual Scripting system, a replacement for the earlier Kismet visual scripting system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gamasutra_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-gamasutra-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">Reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unreal_Engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Licensees">Licensees</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unreal_Engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Licensees">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Unreal engine technology is licensed to many notable entities in the fields of education,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> training simulation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wiredUE4_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-wiredUE4-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-popular_mechanics_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-popular_mechanics-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> construction simulation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> virtual reality,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> and CG animation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Licensees include many universities, corporations, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">U.S. Army</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">U.S. Air Force</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Federal Bureau of Investigation">Federal Bureau of Investigation</a> (FBI),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-50">[50]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Department_of_Transportation" title="Michigan Department of Transportation">Michigan Department of Transportation</a> (MDOT),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wiredUE4_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-wiredUE4-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-popular_mechanics_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-popular_mechanics-5">[5]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/DHS_Science_and_Technology_Directorate" title="DHS Science and Technology Directorate">The Science and Technology Directorate</a> (DHS S&amp;T).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards_and_accolades">Awards and accolades</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unreal_Engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Awards and accolades">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Unreal Engine 3 received the <i><a href="/wiki/Game_Developer_(magazine)" title="Game Developer (magazine)">Game Developer Magazine</a></i> Front Line Awards for "Best Game Engine" (2004,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> 2005,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> 2006,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> 2007,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> Hall of Fame,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> 2009,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> 2010,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> 2011,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> 2012<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>). It received <a href="/wiki/IGN" title="IGN">IGN</a>'s Best of E3 2005 for "Technological Excellence",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Develop_(magazine)" title="Develop (magazine)">Develop Industry Excellence Awards</a> for "Best Game Engine" (2009,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> 2010,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> 2011<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup>).</p>
<p>Unreal Engine 4 received <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GamesRadar" title="GamesRadar">GamesRadar</a>'s E3 2012 Important Stuff Awards for "Best Taste of Next-Gen".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/IGN" title="IGN">IGN</a>'s Best of E3 2012 for "Coolest Tech",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Game_Informer" title="Game Informer">Game Informer</a>'s Best of E3 2012 Awards for "Best Tech",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> and was declared "Best Game Engine" in <a href="/wiki/Develop_(magazine)" title="Develop (magazine)">Develop 100: The Tech List</a> 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> It also received the Develop Industry Excellence Awards for "Best Game Engine" (2013,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> 2016<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup>)</p>
<p>In 2014, the Unreal technology received the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guinness_World_Record" title="Guinness World Record">Guinness World Record</a> award for "Most Successful Videogame Engine".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p>

<div class="refbegin columns references-column-count references-column-count-2" style="-moz-column-count: 2; -webkit-column-count: 2; column-count: 2;">
<ul>
<li>Keith Stuart (December 2009). <i><a class="external text" href="https://www.theguardian.com/technology/gamesblog/2009/dec/14/games-gameculture" rel="nofollow">The hidden story of the 3D engine - by the people who write them.
